          <div class="Pos-r Z-0 article-container" data-template="desktop" id="article-108274953514" style="background-color:white;min-height:315px;">
           <div class="Pos-r sticky-container print-article" style="min-height:315px;">
            <div class="article-inner-container" style="max-width:1280px;">
             <div class="inset-container Mx-a">
              <div class="GridTable">
               <div class="D-tbr row-template">
                <div class="GridTable-Cell Va-t col-left">
                 <div class="content" style="width:825px;min-width:825px;">
                  <noscript>
                  </noscript>
                  <noscript>
                  </noscript>
                  <div class="GridTable grid-content">
                   <div class="D-tbr row-content">
                    <div class="GridTable-Cell Va-t col-left Pend-20" style="width:305px;max-width:305px;">
                     <h1 class="Font-A-6-110 title W-100" id="article-aft-title-108274953514">
                      The Final Step That Takes Your Salad From Good to Great
                     </h1>
                     <div class="attribution-component D-b W-100" id="article-aft-attribution-108274953514" style="max-width:305px;">
                      <div class="author-info author-info-text-first Pt-10 W-100 Bd-t Bd-b Bdstart-0 Bdend-0">
                       <div class="GridTable W-100">
                        <div class="D-tbr">
                         <div class="GridTable-Cell Va-t">
                          <cite class="byline col D-b Fs-n" style="padding-bottom:15px;">
                           <span class="author-name Font-C-2-140 D-b">
                            <a class="attribution-name Font-C-2-140 Fw-b Whs-n" data-ylk="t1:a2;t2:article;t3:cat;t8:51fe7311-88b9-3557-8748-755ff31f986d;t9:17;elm:itm;elmt:link;itc:0" href="http://www.epicurious.com" style="color:#000;" target="_blank">
                             Epicurious
                            </a>
                           </span>
                           <time class="date Font-C-2-140 Fw-n D-b">
                            <span>
                             January 22, 2015
                            </span>
                           </time>
                          </cite>
                         </div>
                        </div>
                       </div>
                      </div>
                     </div>
                    </div>
                    <div class="GridTable-Cell Va-t col-right" style="max-width:500px;">
                     <div class="text-body-wrapper W-100 Font-B-1-160">
                      <noscript>
                      </noscript>
                      <div class="text-body W-100 Font-B-1-160" id="article-aft-text-body-108274953514" style="color:#222222;max-width:500px;">
                       <div class="body read-more-cut">
                        <div class="pre-cut" style="height:auto;margin-bottom:1.5em;">
                         <p>
                          <em>
                           by David Tamarkin
                          </em>
                         </p>
                         <p>
                          <img alt="The Final Step That Takes Your Salad From Good to Great" height="668" src="https://s.yimg.com/cd/resizer/2.0/FIT_TO_WIDTH-w500/a87139c992a53979108880e14a11720c6f7ac778.jpg" width="500"/>
                         </p>
                         <div>
                          <div>
                           <p>
                            I started last night's salad the way I start all of them: With the dressing. I whisked olive oil, mustard, and lemon juice in a big steel bowl (the better to hold all of the salad ingredients). I had some Japanese mayonnaise in the fridge, so I put a little of that for a bit of creaminess, too. I seasoned,Iwhisked. And as the dressing turned silky, I seasoned some more. Then I piled in the chopped romaine and tossed it.
                           </p>
                           <p>
                            Next: An avocado, sliced and beautifully fanned out over top. (Actually, thats a lie. I sort of threw those slices haphazardly over the lettuce. I was eating alone, what do I care about an avocado fan?)Finally, a handful of pepitas and some Microplaned parm. I started eating the salad right out of the bowl.
                           </p>
                           <p>
                            <strong>
                             See more:
                            </strong>
                            <a data-ylk="elm:itm;elmt:link;t1:a2;t2:article;t3:body;t8:51fe7311-88b9-3557-8748-755ff31f986d;t9:18;itc:0" href="http://www.epicurious.com/articlesguides/diningtravel/culinarytravel/best-coffee-shops-in-america?mbid=synd_yfood" target="_blank">
                             25 Coffee Shops You Need to Try
                            </a>
                            <span>
                            </span>
                           </p>
                           <p>
                            But one bite in, I stopped. It wasnt right.
                           </p>
                           <p>
                            It needed salt.
                           </p>
                           <p>
                            Not in the dressingthat was fine. And not in the
                            <em>
                             pepitas
                            </em>
                            (which were salted). Just a final sprinkling over the top of the salad to give it life.
                           </p>
                          </div>
                          <div>
                           <p>
                            Sending a dish to the table with a final sprinkle of salt and/or drizzle of olive oil is always a smart move, but though we usually remember to do it with a pan-roasted chicken breast or a platter of roasted vegetables,saladsusuallyget overlooked.
                           </p>
                           <p>
                            Maybe we forget to give salads a final seasoning because we toss them in highly seasoned dressings, which in theory cover any salt needs. But in the case of my salad last nightand many salads you have probably made, toothere were ingredients that were on top of the salad rather than tossed in. Thats how I like it. But that means my avocados were unseasoned, and even a salty pumpkin seed, or a flake of parm, couldnt bring the salad together.But salt? Salt is the great unifier. And few dishes need as much unifying as salads do.
                           </p>
                           <p>
                            <strong>
                             <strong>
                              See more:
                             </strong>
                            </strong>
                            <a data-ylk="elm:itm;elmt:link;t1:a2;t2:article;t3:body;t8:51fe7311-88b9-3557-8748-755ff31f986d;t9:18;itc:0" href="http://www.epicurious.com/articlesguides/diningtravel/restaurants/the_best_pizza_intro?mbid=synd_yfood" target="_blank">
                             Pizza to Try Before You Die
                            </a>
                           </p>
                           <p>
                            Salting salads also comes with a bonus: A tiny layer of texture. A big, flaky finishing salt like Maldon gives a salad moments of crunch. Even table salt, sprinkled on top at the last minute, can add a microcrunch, if you eat your salad quickly enough. Which, if youve given it that final sprinkling of salt, you will.
                           </p>
                          </div>
                         </div>
                         <p>
                          <strong>
                           More fromEpicurious:
                          </strong>
                         </p>

\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EToday&#x2019;s cake is a swirly and tart lemon cheesecake baked by Kylie Antolini of \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fthebakingbird.com\u002F\"\u003EThe Baking Bird\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&#xA0;blog. Originally from Santa Cruz, California, Antolini is now living in Portland where she works as a dental hygienist by day. At night, she experiments in the kitchen and bakes up&#xA0;\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fthebakingbird.com\u002Fno-bake\u002Fno-bake-chocolate-peanut-butter-oreo-tart\u002F\"\u003Eone-of-a-kind tarts\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fthebakingbird.com\u002Fpie\u002Frosemary-bourbon-peach-pie\u002F\"\u003Ebeautiful pies\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, and \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fthebakingbird.com\u002Fbread-recipes\u002Fsimple-banana-bread\u002F\"\u003Efresh breads\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. Which brings us to her bright lemon lavender dessert.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&#x201C;It&#x2019;s no secret I want to infuse practically everything I can get my hands on with lavender,&#x201D; she writes. &#x201C;With lemons being in season, everyone loving lemon in cheesecake, and the fact that lavender and lemon are a wonderful marriage of flavors, I decided to test this out.&#x201D;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAntolini starts by combining lemon zest and juice with sugar and eggs in a sauce pan and then cooks everything down into a thick curd. She then creates a graham cracker crust on the bottom of a springform pan and fills it with a cheesecake mixture made of ground lavender, Greek yogurt, vanilla, and cream cheese. To create the marble-like design, she spoons the lemon curd over the mixture and runs a knife through it, making yellow and white swirls. It then bakes in the oven and goes right into the fridge for four hours. The finished product? Add tofu, edamame, and &#x2014; my favorite &#x2014; avocado, and you have a totally satisfying meat-free meal.&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E4 cups cooked pearl or hulled barley*\u003Cbr\u003E1 &#xBD; cups shelled, organic edamame\u003Cbr\u003E2 cups wild baby arugula\u003Cbr\u003E2 blocks savory, baked, organic tofu (firm or extra-firm), cut into &#xBE;-inch cubes\u003Cbr\u003E1 ripe avocado, halved and thinly sliced\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003ELemon Tamari Dressing\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E4 tablespoons low-sodium tamari soy sauce\u003Cbr\u003E2 tablespoons toasted sesame oil\u003Cbr\u003EJuice of 1 lemon\u003Cbr\u003E1 tablespoon dried oregano\u003Cbr\u003E1 teaspoon finely grated lemon zest (optional)\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn a large bowl, whisk together the tamari soy sauce, sesame oil, lemon juice, oregano, and lemon zest until well combined. Add slightly cooled cooked barley to the bowl and toss to coat.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAdd the shelled edamame and arugula and toss gently to combine. To serve, divide the barley salad among four bowls and top with tofu and avocado slices.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E*Hulled barley is barley in its most natural state, which means it needs to cook for quite a bit longer than pearl barley &#x2014;1 to 1 &#xBD; hours longer. Pearl barley has the hull removed and is therefore less tough. Adjust your cooking time based on the variety you Meal Kits Help Small Farmers Find New Ways to Distribute Food?","summary":"A horde of new, sleek, venture capital-funded services has arrived on the market peddling what they think is a solution: kits made of raw ingredients  portioned and sometimes prepped  that can be assembled quickly to make a meal from scratch.","body":"\u003Cfigure class=\"tmblr-full\"\u003E\n \u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w540\u002Fcf2d8a67b35ba526e60d2561d5701207687368a6.jpg\" alt=\"Can Meal Kits Help Small Farmers Find New Ways to Distribute Food?\" width=\"540\" height=\"360\"\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ci\u003EA tamale kit from Luke&#x2019;s Local.\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ci\u003EBy Anna Roth, Civil Eats\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EPutting food on the table on a busy weeknight is a universal problem. Now, a horde of new, sleek, venture capital-funded services has arrived on the market peddling what they think is a solution: kits made of raw ingredients &#x2014; portioned and sometimes prepped &#x2014; that can be assembled quickly to make a meal &#x201C;from scratch.&#x201D;\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe rising popularity of these meal kits has led to a Gold Rush of sorts. Big brands like \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.blueapron.com\u002F\"\u003EBlue Apron\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.plated.com\u002F\"\u003EPlated\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, and \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.hellofresh.com\u002F\"\u003EHello Fresh\u003C\u002Fa\u003E cater to the general population, each offering a half dozen or so meal plans for everyone from families to vegetarians. Then there are the niche kits: organic food (\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Featpurely.com\u002F\"\u003EEat Purely\u003C\u002Fa\u003E), vegan food (\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.thepurplecarrot.com\u002F\"\u003EThe Purple Carrot\u003C\u002Fa\u003E), Southern cooking (\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.peachdish.com\u002F\"\u003EPeachDish\u003C\u002Fa\u003E), and smoothies (\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fgreenblender.com\u002F\"\u003EGreen Blender\u003C\u002Fa\u003E). All claim to work with local suppliers to some extent, creating a whole new distribution outlet for small- to mid-size farms.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAlthough these kits have the potential to waste a lot of packaging, many of the companies behind them actually hope to cut down on food waste. Instead of buying obscure ingredients you&#x2019;ll only use once, or receiving three heads of kale in your weekly community supported agriculture (CSA) subscription box that will inevitably wilt in the crisper drawer, the kits provide you with only what you need for a recipe. Not a bad model for a country that throws away \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fcivileats.com\u002F2014\u002F10\u002F07\u002Funcovering-americas-food-waste-fiasco\u002F\"\u003E35 million tons\u003C\u002Fa\u003E of food a year.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fcivileats.com\u002F2015\u002F06\u002F25\u002Fsearching-for-food-you-can-believe-in-eat-well-guides-new-app-has-you-covered\u002F#sthash.ufXlJDnq.dpuf\"\u003EMore: Searching for Food You Can Believe in? Eat Well Guide&#x2019;s New App Has You Covered&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cfigure class=\"tmblr-full\"\u003E\n \u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fg\u002Fimages\u002Fspaceball.gif\" alt=\"image\" width=\"540\" height=\"360\" data-src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w540\u002Ff54143c1b1ecee723b1ea581f528876397afc086.jpg\"\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ci\u003EAn assortment of meal kits from Blue Apron.\u003C\u002Fi\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ESome meal kit creators also strive to eliminate food waste higher in the supply chain. Blue Apron claims to deliver more than 3 million meals a month. That requires a lot of produce, and Blue Apron and other services are collaborating with farmers, in some cases planning menus a year out in order to sync with crop rotations, and ensure that all the food each farm grows goes to use. In doing so, they could be creating a new form of farm-to-table food delivery, somewhere in between a CSA and a restaurant.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&#x201C;We&#x2019;ve had a farm strategy since day one,&#x201D; says Matthew Wadiak, founder and COO of Blue Apron. He says that the three-year-old company has spent the past few years talking with farmers, assessing their needs and challenges, then figuring out how to address them. Wadiak is a champion of forgotten heirloom produce, like \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.highmowingseeds.com\u002Forganic-non-gmo-seeds-honey-nut-butternut-squash.html\"\u003Ehoneynut squash\u003C\u002Fa\u003E and \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fblack%20panther%20soybeans\u002F\"\u003Eblack panther soybeans\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, which he says goes over well with both his customers and the farmers.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fcivileats.com\u002F2015\u002F02\u002F12\u002Fis-the-online-grocery-boom-good-for-the-planet\u002F\"\u003E\u003Cb\u003EMore: Is The Online Grocery Boom Good for the Planet?\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EBlue Apron works with the 31 farms, and the culinary staff have sustainable agriculture backgrounds (the culinary director came from \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.stonebarnscenter.org\u002F\"\u003EStone Barns Center for Food and Agriculture\u003C\u002Fa\u003E), so an awareness of crop rotation and cover crops has been built into their business model.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFor example, the company recently worked with 12-acre Alewife Farm farm and others nearby in New York&#x2019;s Hudson Valley to source thousands of pounds of pea shoots for a recent \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.blueapron.com\u002Frecipes\u002Fseared-wild-king-salmon-with-preserved-lemon-quinoa\"\u003Eseared salmon dish\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fcivileats.com\u002F2014\u002F06\u002F16\u002Fstarting-out-a-new-farmers-first-growing-season\u002F\"\u003EAlewife owner Tyler Dennis\u003C\u002Fa\u003E says that the peas helped fix nitrogen in his soil, and the guaranteed revenue from the order was a huge boon.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fcivileats.com\u002F2015\u002F02\u002F06\u002Fhow-can-we-get-america-cooking-one-crumb-at-a-time\u002F#sthash.Se8xPhyJ.dpuf\"\u003EMore:&#xA0;How Can We Get America Cooking? One Crumb at a Time\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fb\u003E&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFarmers are not only reliant on the weather, but also on the whims of customers at farmers&#x2019; markets and the picky palates of chefs to sell their wares. Dennis is talking with the company to grow more peas, as well as some baby fennel and baby leeks. &#x201C;I would really like to incorporate my business with Blue Apron&#x2019;s in a more substantial way,&#x201D; he says.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThese meal kits can be expensive when compared to a home-cooked meal at around $8 and $12 per person. That&#x2019;s not much more than take-out from most casual restaurants, but they do require 10-30 minutes of actual cooking time. They also create their own waste in the form of the packaging that holds, say, two tablespoons of butter for one recipe, or the ice packs to keep the food cold. But meal kit proponents are quick to point out that they&#x2019;re also eliminating a lot of the packaging higher up in the food distribution stream.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EMore: An App for That: Technology as a Solution to Our Broken Food System?&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EPerhaps the most limiting part, at least to someone who knows how to cook, is that these services only offer a few options each day. That&#x2019;s where a model more like \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Flukeslocal.com\u002F\"\u003ELuke&#x2019;s Local\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, a San Francisco-based meal delivery company, comes in. Its stated goal is to help busy people get through the week. So the business offers recipe kits, but also heat-and-serve prepared meals and raw produce and ingredients, more like a regular CSA.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&#x201C;[Our customers] love that they can get some of these things to cook dinner or have dinner at home, some fruit for the morning for breakfast, a box lunch to bring to work,&#x201D; says owner Luke Chappell.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fcivileats.com\u002F2014\u002F11\u002F17\u002Ftoss-those-take-out-menus-new-study-says-cooking-makes-us-healthier\u002F#sthash.Il10pdyd.dpuf\"\u003E\u003Cb\u003EMore: Toss Those Take-Out Menus: New Study Says Cooking Makes us Healthier\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ELike Wadiak, Chappell has made working with farmers a priority from the beginning. He says that there&#x2019;s rarely a day that goes by without communication between his team of chefs and farmers like Andy Griffin from Watsonville&#x2019;s \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.mariquita.com\u002F\"\u003EMariquita Farm\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&#x2014;either they&#x2019;re on the phone talking about what&#x2019;s fresh that week, or Griffin is texting them photos of what&#x2019;s ripe and good to feature. In a weekly meeting, the team looks at all the produce set to come in and figures out what to do with it.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EOffering a range of options also eliminates waste almost entirely, says Chappell. For example, he can source a whole pig from Chico, California-based \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fllanoseco.com\u002F\"\u003ELlano Seco Ranch\u003C\u002Fa\u003E and use every part: the skin for chicharones, the bones for broth, the cuts for the prepared chefs meals, chorizo for a breakfast burrito, and tenderloins for a dinner recipe kit.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fcivileats.com\u002F2013\u002F10\u002F14\u002Fcooking-as-the-cornerstone-of-a-sustainable-food-system\u002F#sthash.VVJ03odz.dpuf\"\u003E\u003Cb\u003ECooking as the Cornerstone of a Sustainable Food System\u003C\u002Fb\u003E\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFood gurus like Michael Pollan and Mark Bittman often point to scratch cooking as \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Ftime.com\u002F3483888\u002Fthe-truth-about-home-cooking\u002F\"\u003Ethe answer\u003C\u002Fa\u003E to many of \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.nytimes.com\u002F2009\u002F08\u002F02\u002Fmagazine\u002F02cooking-t.html?pagewanted=all&amp;_r=0\"\u003Eour food system&#x2019;s ills\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, and the best a way to eat sustainably grown ingredients without breaking the bank. But it&#x2019;s unclear whether or not meal kits can ever serve as a bridge to more affordable, traditional home cooking.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThey could simply be a function of the new &#x201C;\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fmedium.com\u002Fondemand\u002Fthe-shut-in-economy-ec3ec1294816\"\u003Eshut in economy\u003C\u002Fa\u003E,&#x201D; where those with means order everything into their homes and never leave. But those behind the companies say they&#x2019;re working to make it easier for busy professionals and families, who might not cook at all otherwise, to take advantage of the values of community supported agriculture or farmers&#x2019; market shopping without the time commitment.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E&#x201C;My argument is that we take the good parts of the CSA, which is the seasonality, but we make it really usable for everybody,&#x201D; says Wadiak. And no matter how you feel about the cooking-by-the-numbers approach to dinner, it&#x2019;s hard to argue with that.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cb\u003EMore food for thought from Yahoo Drinks That Do (and Taste) Good\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ca Final Step That Takes Your Salad From Good to Great","summary":"There's just one trick you need to know to have better salads every time. Do you know it?","body":"\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003Eby David Tamarkin\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fs.yimg.com\u002Fcd\u002Fresizer\u002F2.0\u002FFIT_TO_WIDTH-w500\u002Fa87139c992a53979108880e14a11720c6f7ac778.jpg\" width=\"500\" height=\"668\" alt=\"The Final Step That Takes Your Salad From Good to Great\"\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cdiv\u003E \n \u003Cdiv\u003E \n  \u003Cp\u003EI started last night&apos;s salad the way I start all of them: With the dressing. I whisked olive oil, mustard, and lemon juice in a big steel bowl (the better to hold all of the salad ingredients). I had some Japanese mayonnaise in the fridge, so I put a little of that for a bit of creaminess, too. I seasoned,&#xA0;I&#xA0;whisked. And as the dressing turned silky, I seasoned some more. Then I piled in the chopped romaine and tossed it.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n  \u003Cp\u003ENext: An avocado, sliced and beautifully fanned out over top. (Actually, that&#x2019;s a lie. I sort of threw those slices haphazardly over the lettuce. I was eating alone, what do I care about an avocado fan?)&#xA0;Finally, a handful of pepitas and some Microplaned parm. I started eating the salad right out of the bowl.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n  \u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ESee more:&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.epicurious.com\u002Farticlesguides\u002Fdiningtravel\u002Fculinarytravel\u002Fbest-coffee-shops-in-america?mbid=synd_yfood\"\u003E25 Coffee Shops You Need to Try\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cspan\u003E&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n  \u003Cp\u003EBut one bite in, I stopped. It wasn&#x2019;t right.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n  \u003Cp\u003EIt needed salt.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n  \u003Cp\u003ENot in the dressing&#x2014;that was fine. And not in the&#xA0;\u003Cem\u003Epepitas\u003C\u002Fem\u003E&#xA0;(which were salted). Just a final sprinkling over the top of the salad to give it life.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n \u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E \n \u003Cdiv\u003E \n  \u003Cp\u003ESending a dish to the table with a final sprinkle of salt and\u002For drizzle of olive oil is always a smart move, but though we usually remember to do it with a pan-roasted chicken breast or a platter of roasted vegetables,&#xA0;salads&#xA0;usually&#xA0;get overlooked.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n  \u003Cp\u003EMaybe we forget to give salads a final seasoning because we toss them in highly seasoned dressings, which in theory cover any salt needs. But in the case of my salad last night&#x2014;and many salads you have probably made, too&#x2014;there were ingredients that were on top of the salad rather than tossed in. That&#x2019;s how I like it. But that means my avocados were unseasoned, and even a salty pumpkin seed, or a flake of parm, couldn&#x2019;t bring the salad together.&#xA0;But salt? Salt is the great unifier. And few dishes need as much unifying as salads do.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n  \u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ESee more:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E&#xA0;\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.epicurious.com\u002Farticlesguides\u002Fdiningtravel\u002Frestaurants\u002Fthe_best_pizza_intro?mbid=synd_yfood\"\u003EPizza to Try Before You Die\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n  \u003Cp\u003ESalting salads also comes with a bonus: A tiny layer of texture. A big, flaky finishing salt like Maldon gives a salad moments of crunch. Even table salt, sprinkled on top at the last minute, can add a microcrunch, if you eat your salad quickly enough. Which, if you&#x2019;ve given it that final sprinkling of salt, you will.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n \u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E \n\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EMore from&#xA0;Epicurious:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.epicurious.com\u002Farticlesguides\u002Fdiningtravel\u002Frestaurants\u002Fbreakfast-egg-sandwich?mbid=synd_yfood\"\u003EWinter Breakfast of Champions: Creating the
